   (disambiguation) and [351]Albert Einstein (disambiguation).

     * [448]v
     * [449]t
     * [450]e

   Albert Einstein ([451]/ˈaɪnstaɪn/, [452]EYEN-styne;^[453][4]
   German: [454][ˈalbɛʁt ˈʔaɪnʃtaɪn] ^[455]ⓘ; 14 March 1879 – 18 April
   1955) was a German-born [456]theoretical physicist who is best known
   for developing the [457]theory of relativity. Einstein also made
   important contributions to [458]quantum mechanics.^[459][1]^[460][5]
   His [461]mass–energy equivalence formula [462]E = mc^2, which arises
   from [463]special relativity, has been called "the world's most famous
   equation".^[464][6] He received the 1921 [465]Nobel Prize in Physics
   for "his services to theoretical physics, and especially for his
   discovery of the law of the [466]photoelectric effect".^[467][7]

   Born in the [468]German Empire, Einstein moved to Switzerland in 1895,
   forsaking his [469]German citizenship the following year. In 1897, at
   the age of seventeen he enrolled in the mathematics and physics
   teaching diploma program at the Swiss [470]Federal Polytechnic School
   in [471]Zurich, graduating in 1900. He acquired [472]Swiss citizenship
   a year later and afterwards secured a permanent position at the
   [473]Swiss Patent Office in [474]Bern. In 1905, he submitted a
   successful PhD dissertation to the [475]University of Zurich. In 1914,
   he moved to [476]Berlin to join the [477]Prussian Academy of Sciences
   and the [478]Humboldt University of Berlin, becoming director of the
   [479]Kaiser Wilhelm Institute for Physics. In 1933, while Einstein was
   visiting the United States, [480]Adolf Hitler came to power in Germany.
   Horrified by the [481]Nazi persecution of his fellow Jews,^[482][8] he
   decided to remain in the US, and was granted [483]American citizenship
   in 1940.^[484][9] On the eve of [485]World War II, he endorsed [486]a
   letter to President [487]Franklin D. Roosevelt alerting him to the
   potential [488]German nuclear weapons program and recommending that the
   US begin [489]similar research.

   In 1905, he published [490]four groundbreaking papers, sometimes
   described as his [491]annus mirabilis (miracle year).^[492][10] These
   papers outlined a theory of the photoelectric effect, explained
   [493]Brownian motion, introduced his special theory of relativity, and
   demonstrated that if the special theory is correct, mass and energy are
   equivalent to each other. In 1915, he proposed a [494]general theory of
   relativity that extended his system of mechanics to incorporate
   [495]gravitation. A cosmological paper that he published the following
   year laid out the implications of general relativity for the modeling
   of the structure and evolution of the [496]universe as a
   whole.^[497][11]^[498][12] In 1917, Einstein wrote a paper which laid
   the foundations for the concepts of both [499]laser and [500]maser, and
   contained a trove of information that would be beneficial to
   developments in physics later on, such as [501]quantum electrodynamics
   and [502]quantum optics.^[503][13] A joint paper in 1935, with
   physicist [504]Nathan Rosen, introduced the notion of a
   [505]wormhole.^[506][14]

   In the middle part of his career, Einstein made important contributions
   to [507]statistical mechanics and quantum theory. Especially notable
   was his work on the quantum physics of [508]radiation, in which light
   consists of particles, subsequently called [509]photons. With physicist
   [510]Satyendra Nath Bose, he laid the groundwork for [511]Bose-Einstein
   statistics. For much of the last phase of his academic life, Einstein
   worked on two endeavors that ultimately proved unsuccessful. First, he
   advocated against quantum theory's introduction of fundamental
   randomness into science's picture of the world, objecting that
   "God does not play dice".^[512][15] Second, he attempted to devise a
   [513]unified field theory by generalizing his geometric theory of
   gravitation to include [514]electromagnetism. As a result, he became
   increasingly isolated from mainstream [515]modern physics. In 1999, he
   was named [516]Time's [517]Person of the Century.^[518][16]

Life and career

Childhood, youth and education

   See also: [519]Einstein family

   [520]A young boy with short hair and a round face, wearing a white
   collar and large bow, with vest, coat, skirt, and high boots. He is
   leaning against an ornate chair. Einstein in 1882, age 3

   Albert Einstein was born in [521]Ulm,^[522][17] in the Kingdom of
   Württemberg in the German Empire, on 14 March 1879.^[523][18] His
   parents, secular [524]Ashkenazi Jews, were [525]Hermann Einstein, a
   salesman and engineer, and [526]Pauline Koch. In 1880, the family moved
   to [527]Munich's borough of [528]Ludwigsvorstadt-Isarvorstadt, where
   Einstein's father and his uncle Jakob founded Elektrotechnische Fabrik
   J. Einstein & Cie, a company that manufactured electrical equipment
   based on [529]direct current.^[530][17] He often related a formative
   event from his youth, when he was sick in bed and his father brought
   him a [531]compass. This sparked his lifelong fascination with
   [532]electromagnetism. He realized that "Something deeply hidden had to
   be behind things."^[533][19]

   Albert attended St. Peter's [534]Catholic elementary school in Munich
   from the age of five. When he was eight, he was transferred to the
   [535]Luitpold Gymnasium, where he received advanced primary and then
   secondary school education.^[536][20]

   Einstein's parents, [537]Hermann and [538]Pauline

   In 1894, Hermann and Jakob's company tendered for a contract to install
   electric lighting in Munich, but without success—they lacked the
   capital that would have been required to update their technology from
   direct current to the more efficient, [539]alternating current
   alternative.^[540][21] The failure of their bid forced them to sell
   their Munich factory and search for new opportunities elsewhere. The
   Einstein family moved to Italy, first to [541]Milan and a few months
   later to [542]Pavia, where they settled in [543]Palazzo
   Cornazzani.^[544][22] Einstein, then fifteen, stayed behind in Munich
   in order to finish his schooling. His father wanted him to study
   [545]electrical engineering, but he was a fractious pupil who found the
   Gymnasium's regimen and teaching methods far from congenial. He later
   wrote that the school's policy of strict [546]rote learning was harmful
   to creativity. At the end of December 1894, a letter from a doctor
   persuaded the Luitpold's authorities to release him from its care, and
   he joined his family in Pavia.^[547][23] While in Italy as a teenager,
   he wrote an essay entitled "On the Investigation of the State of the
   [548]Ether in a Magnetic Field".^[549][24]^[550][25]

   Einstein excelled at physics and mathematics from an early age, and
   soon acquired the mathematical expertise normally only found in a child
   several years his senior. He began teaching himself algebra, calculus
   and [551]Euclidean geometry when he was twelve; he made such rapid
   progress that he discovered an original proof of the [552]Pythagorean
   theorem before his thirteenth birthday.^[553][26]^[554][27]^[555][28] A
   family tutor, [556]Max Talmud, said that only a short time after he had
   given the twelve year old Einstein a geometry textbook, the boy
   "had worked through the whole book. He thereupon devoted himself to
   higher mathematics ... Soon the flight of his mathematical genius was
   so high I could not follow."^[557][29] Einstein recorded that he had
   "mastered [558]integral and [559]differential calculus" while still
   just fourteen.^[560][27] His love of algebra and geometry was so great
   that at twelve, he was already confident that nature could be
   understood as a "mathematical structure".^[561][29] [562]Studio photo
   of a boy seated in a relaxed posture and wearing a suit, posed in front
   of a backdrop of scenery. Einstein in 1893, age 14

   At thirteen, when his range of enthusiasms had broadened to include
   music and philosophy,^[563][30] Talmud introduced Einstein to
   [564]Kant's [565]Critique of Pure Reason. Kant became his favorite
   philosopher; according to Talmud,
   "At the time he was still a child, only thirteen years old, yet Kant's
   works, incomprehensible to ordinary mortals, seemed to be clear to
   him."^[566][29]

   In 1895, at the age of sixteen, Einstein sat the entrance examination
   for the [567]Federal Polytechnic School (later the Eidgenössische
   Technische Hochschule, ETH) in Zurich, Switzerland. He failed to reach
   the required standard in the general part of the test,^[568][31] but
   performed with distinction in physics and mathematics.^[569][32] On the
   advice of the polytechnic's principal, he completed his secondary
   education at the [570]Argovian cantonal school (a [571]gymnasium) in
   [572]Aarau, Switzerland, graduating in 1896.^[573][33] While lodging in
   Aarau with the family of [574]Jost Winteler, he fell in love with
   Winteler's daughter, Marie. (His sister, [575]Maja, later married
   Winteler's son Paul.^[576][34])
   [577]Einstein's matriculation certificate at the age of 17. The heading
   translates as "The Education Committee of the Canton of Aargau". His
   scores were German 5, French 3, Italian 5, History 6, Geography 4,
   Algebra 6, Geometry 6, Descriptive Geometry 6, Physics 6, Chemistry 5,
   Natural History 5, Art Drawing 4, Technical Drawing 4. 6 = very good, 5
   = good, 4 = sufficient, 3 = insufficient, 2 = poor, 1 = very poor.
   Einstein's [578]Matura certificate from canton [579]Aargau,
   1896^[580][note 2]

   In January 1896, with his father's approval, Einstein renounced his
   [581]citizenship of the German Kingdom of Württemberg in order to avoid
   [582]conscription into military service.^[583][35] The [584]Matura
   (graduation for the successful completion of higher secondary
   schooling), awarded to him in September 1896, acknowledged him to have
   performed well across most of the curriculum, allotting him a [585]top
   grade of 6 for history, physics, algebra, geometry, and descriptive
   geometry.^[586][36] At seventeen, he enrolled in the four-year
   mathematics and physics teaching diploma program at the Federal
   Polytechnic School. Marie Winteler, a year older than him, took up a
   teaching post in [587]Olsberg, Switzerland.^[588][34]

   The five other polytechnic school freshmen following the same course as
   Einstein included just one woman, a twenty year old [589]Serbian,
   [590]Mileva Marić. Over the next few years, the pair spent many hours
   discussing their shared interests and learning about topics in physics
   that the polytechnic school's lectures did not cover. In his letters to
   Marić, Einstein confessed that exploring science with her by his side
   was much more enjoyable than reading a textbook in solitude. Eventually
   the two students became not only friends but also lovers.^[591][37]

   Historians of physics are divided on the question of the extent to
   which Marić contributed to the insights of Einstein's annus mirabilis
   publications. There is at least some evidence that he was influenced by
   her scientific ideas,^[592][37]^[593][38]^[594][39] but there are
   scholars who doubt whether her impact on his thought was of any great
   significance at all.^[595][40]^[596][41]^[597][42]^[598][43]

Marriages, relationships and children

   [599][220px-Albert_Einstein_and_his_wife_Mileva_Maric.jpg] Albert
   Einstein and [600]Mileva Marić Einstein, 1912

   Correspondence between Einstein and Marić, discovered and published in
   1987, revealed that in early 1902, while Marić was visiting her parents
   in [601]Novi Sad, she gave birth to a daughter, [602]Lieserl. When
   Marić returned to Switzerland it was without the child, whose fate is
   uncertain. A letter of Einstein's that he wrote in September 1903
   suggests that the girl was either given up for adoption or died of
   [603]scarlet fever in infancy.^[604][44]^[605][45]

   Einstein and Marić married in January 1903. In May 1904, their son
   [606]Hans Albert was born in [607]Bern, Switzerland. Their son
   [608]Eduard was born in Zurich in July 1910. In letters that Einstein
   wrote to Marie Winteler in the months before Eduard's arrival, he
   described his love for his wife as "misguided" and mourned the "missed
   life" that he imagined he would have enjoyed if he had married Winteler
   instead: "I think of you in heartfelt love every spare minute and am so
   unhappy as only a man can be."^[609][46]
   [610]Einstein, looking relaxed and holding a pipe, stands next to a
   smiling, well-dressed Elsa who is wearing a fancy hat and fur wrap. She
   is looking at him. Albert and [611]Elsa Einstein arriving in New York,
   1921

   In 1912, Einstein entered into a relationship with [612]Elsa Löwenthal,
   who was both his first cousin on his mother's side and his second
   cousin on his father's.^[613][47]^[614][48]^[615][49] When Marić
   learned of his infidelity soon after moving to Berlin with him in April
   1914, she returned to Zurich, taking Hans Albert and Eduard with
   her.^[616][37] Einstein and Marić were granted a divorce on 14 February
   1919 on the grounds of having lived apart for five
   years.^[617][50]^[618][51] As part of the divorce settlement, Einstein
   agreed that if he were to win a Nobel Prize, he would give the money
   that he received to Marić; he won the prize two years later.^[619][52]

   Einstein married Löwenthal in 1919.^[620][53]^[621][54] In 1923, he
   began a relationship with a secretary named Betty Neumann, the niece of
   his close friend Hans Mühsam.^[622][55]^[623][56]^[624][57]^[625][58]
   Löwenthal nevertheless remained loyal to him, accompanying him when he
   emigrated to the United States in 1933. In 1935, she was diagnosed with
   heart and kidney problems. She died in December 1936.^[626][59]
   [627][220px-Albert_Einstein_and_Elsa_Einstein_arriving_by_ship%2C_San_D
   iego%2C_1930_%28cropped%29.jpg] Albert and Elsa Einstein, 1930

   A volume of Einstein's letters released by [628]Hebrew University of
   Jerusalem in 2006^[629][60] added some other women with whom he was
   romantically involved. They included Margarete Lebach (a married
   Austrian),^[630][61] Estella Katzenellenbogen (the rich owner of a
   florist business), Toni Mendel (a wealthy Jewish widow) and Ethel
   Michanowski (a Berlin socialite), with whom he spent time and from whom
   he accepted gifts while married to Löwenthal.^[631][62]^[632][63] After
   being widowed, Einstein was briefly in a relationship with Margarita
   Konenkova, thought by some to be a Russian spy; her husband, the
   Russian sculptor [633]Sergei Konenkov, created the bronze bust of
   Einstein at the [634]Institute for Advanced Study at
   Princeton.^[635][64]^[636][65]

   Following an episode of acute mental illness at about the age of
   twenty, Einstein's son Eduard was diagnosed with
   [637]schizophrenia.^[638][66] He spent the remainder of his life either
   in the care of his mother or in temporary confinement in an asylum.
   After her death, he was committed permanently to [639]Burghölzli, the
   Psychiatric University Hospital in Zurich.^[640][67]

1902–1909: Assistant at the Swiss Patent Office

   [641]Head and shoulders shot of a young, moustached man with dark,
   curly hair wearing a plaid suit and vest, striped shirt, and a dark
   tie. Einstein at the [642]Swiss patent office, 1904

   Einstein graduated from the Federal Polytechnic School in 1900, duly
   certified as competent to teach mathematics and physics.^[643][68] His
   successful acquisition of Swiss citizenship in February 1901^[644][69]
   was not followed by the usual sequel of [645]conscription; the Swiss
   authorities deemed him medically unfit for military service. He found
   that Swiss schools too appeared to have no use for him, failing to
   offer him a teaching position despite the almost two years that he
   spent applying for one. Eventually it was with the help of [646]Marcel
   Grossmann's father that he secured a post in [647]Bern at the
   [648]Swiss Patent Office,^[649][70]^[650][71] as an [651]assistant
   examiner – level III.^[652][72]^[653][73]

   [654]Patent applications that landed on Einstein's desk for his
   evaluation included ideas for a gravel sorter and an electric
   typewriter.^[655][73] His employers were pleased enough with his work
   to make his position permanent in 1903, although they did not think
   that he should be promoted until he had "fully mastered machine
   technology".^[656][74] It is conceivable that his labors at the patent
   office had a bearing on his development of his special theory of
   relativity. He arrived at his revolutionary ideas about space, time and
   light through thought experiments about the transmission of signals and
   the synchronization of clocks, matters which also figured in some of
   the inventions submitted to him for assessment.^[657][10]

   In 1902, Einstein and some friends whom he had met in Bern formed a
   group that held regular meetings to discuss science and philosophy.
   Their choice of a name for their club, the [658]Olympia Academy, was an
   ironic comment upon its far from Olympian status. Sometimes they were
   joined by Marić, who limited her participation in their proceedings to
   careful listening.^[659][75] The thinkers whose works they reflected
   upon included [660]Henri Poincaré, [661]Ernst Mach and [662]David Hume,
   all of whom significantly influenced Einstein's own subsequent ideas
   and beliefs.^[663][76]

1900–1905: First scientific papers

   [664]Cover image of the PhD dissertation of Albert Einstein Einstein's
   1905 dissertation, Eine neue Bestimmung der Moleküldimensione ("A new
   determination of molecular dimensions")

   Einstein's first paper, [665]"Folgerungen aus den
   Capillaritätserscheinungen" ("Conclusions drawn from the phenomena of
   capillarity"), in which he proposed a model of intermolecular
   attraction that he afterwards disavowed as worthless, was published in
   the journal [666]Annalen der Physik in 1901.^[667][77]^[668][78] His
   24-page doctoral dissertation also addressed a topic in molecular
   physics. Titled "Eine neue Bestimmung der Moleküldimensionen" ("A New
   Determination of Molecular Dimensions") and dedicated to his friend
   Marcel Grossman, it was completed on 30 April 1905^[669][79] and
   approved by Professor [670]Alfred Kleiner of the University of Zurich
   three months later. (Einstein was formally awarded his PhD on 15
   January 1906.)^[671][79]^[672][80]^[673][81] Four other pieces of work
   that Einstein completed in 1905—[674]his famous papers on the
   [675]photoelectric effect, [676]Brownian motion, his [677]special
   theory of relativity and the [678]equivalence of mass and energy—have
   led to the year being celebrated as an annus mirabilis for physics akin
   to 1666 (the year in which [679]Isaac Newton experienced his greatest
   epiphanies). The publications deeply impressed Einstein's
   contemporaries.^[680][82]

1908–1933: Academic career in Europe

   Einstein's sabbatical as a civil servant approached its end in 1908,
   when he secured a junior teaching position at the [681]University of
   Bern. In 1909, a lecture on relativistic [682]electrodynamics that he
   gave at the University of Zurich, much admired by Alfred Kleiner, led
   to Zurich's luring him away from Bern with a newly created associate
   professorship.^[683][83] Promotion to a full professorship followed in
   April 1911, when he accepted a chair at the German
   [684]Charles-Ferdinand University in Prague, a move which required him
   to become an [685]Austrian citizen of the [686]Austro-Hungarian Empire,
   which was not completed.^[687][84]^[688][85]^[689][86] His time in
   Prague saw him producing eleven research papers.^[690][87]
   [691][lossy-page1-240px-ETH-BIB-Einstein%2C_Albert_und_Kollegen_am_Phys
   ik-Labor_ETH-Portrait-Portr_10750.tif.jpg] Einstein with colleagues at
   the [692]ETH in [693]Zurich, 1913

   In July 1912, he returned to his alma mater, the [694]ETH Zurich, to
   take up a chair in theoretical physics. His teaching activities there
   centred on [695]thermodynamics and analytical mechanics, and his
   research interests included the molecular theory of heat,
   [696]continuum mechanics and the development of a relativistic theory
   of gravitation. In his work on the latter topic, he was assisted by his
   friend, Marcel Grossmann, whose knowledge of the kind of mathematics
   required was greater than his own.^[697][88]

   In the spring of 1913, two German visitors, [698]Max Planck and
   [699]Walther Nernst, called upon Einstein in Zurich in the hope of
   persuading him to relocate to Berlin.^[700][89] They offered him
   membership of the [701]Prussian Academy of Sciences, the directorship
   of the planned [702]Kaiser Wilhelm Institute for Physics and a chair at
   the [703]Humboldt University of Berlin that would allow him to pursue
   his research supported by a professorial salary but with no teaching
   duties to burden him.^[704][48] Their invitation was all the more
   appealing to him because Berlin happened to be the home of his latest
   girlfriend, Elsa Löwenthal.^[705][89] He duly joined the Academy on 24
   July 1913,^[706][90] and moved into an apartment in the Berlin district
   of [707]Dahlem on 1 April 1914.^[708][48] He was installed in his
   Humboldt University position shortly thereafter.^[709][90]
   [710][240px-Berliner_Physiker_u_Chemiker_1920.jpg] Einstein with other
   physicists and chemists in [711]Berlin, 1920

   The outbreak of the [712]First World War in July 1914 marked the
   beginning of Einstein's gradual estrangement from the nation of his
   birth. When the "[713]Manifesto of the Ninety-Three" was published in
   October 1914—a document signed by a host of prominent German thinkers
   that justified Germany's belligerence—Einstein was one of the few
   German intellectuals to distance himself from it and sign the
   alternative, eirenic "[714]Manifesto to the Europeans"
   instead.^[715][91]^[716][92] However, this expression of his doubts
   about German policy did not prevent him from being elected to a
   two-year term as president of the [717]German Physical Society in
   1916.^[718][93] When the Kaiser Wilhelm Institute for Physics opened
   its doors the following year—its foundation delayed because of the
   war—Einstein was appointed its first director, just as Planck and
   Nernst had promised.^[719][94]

   Einstein was elected a Foreign Member of the [720]Royal Netherlands
   Academy of Arts and Sciences in 1920,^[721][95] and a [722]Foreign
   Member of the Royal Society in 1921. In 1922, he was awarded the 1921
   [723]Nobel Prize in Physics "for his services to Theoretical Physics,
   and especially for his discovery of the law of the photoelectric
   effect".^[724][7] At this point some physicists still regarded the
   general theory of relativity skeptically, and the Nobel citation
   displayed a degree of doubt even about the work on photoelectricity
   that it acknowledged: it did not assent to Einstein's notion of the
   particulate nature of light, which only won over the entire scientific
   community when [725]S. N. Bose derived the [726]Planck spectrum in
   1924. That same year, Einstein was elected an International Honorary
   Member of the [727]American Academy of Arts and Sciences.^[728][96]
   Britain's closest equivalent of the Nobel award, the [729]Royal
   Society's [730]Copley Medal, was not hung around Einstein's neck until
   1925.^[731][1] He was elected an International Member of the
   [732]American Philosophical Society in 1930.^[733][97]

   Einstein resigned from the Prussian Academy in March 1933. His
   accomplishments in Berlin had included the completion of the general
   theory of relativity, proving the [734]Einstein–de Haas effect,
   contributing to the quantum theory of radiation, and the development of
   [735]Bose–Einstein statistics.^[736][48]

1919: Putting general relativity to the test

   [737][220px-19191125_A_New_Physics_Based_on_Einstein_-_The_New_York_Tim
   es.png] The New York Times reported confirmation of the bending of
   light by gravitation after observations (made in [738]Príncipe and
   [739]Sobral) of the 29 May 1919 eclipse were presented to a joint
   meeting in London of the [740]Royal Society and the [741]Royal
   Astronomical Society on 6 November 1919.^[742][98]

   In 1907, Einstein reached a milestone on his long journey from his
   special theory of relativity to a new idea of gravitation with the
   formulation of his [743]equivalence principle, which asserts that an
   observer in an infinitesimally small box falling freely in a
   gravitational field would be unable to find any evidence that the field
   exists. In 1911, he used the principle to estimate the amount by which
   a ray of light from a distant star would be [744]bent by the
   gravitational pull of the Sun as it passed close to the Sun's
   [745]photosphere (that is, the Sun's apparent surface). He reworked his
   calculation in 1913, having now found a way to model gravitation with
   the [746]Riemann curvature tensor of a non-Euclidean four-dimensional
   [747]spacetime. By the fall of 1915, his reimagining of the mathematics
   of gravitation in terms of Riemannian geometry was complete, and he
   applied his new theory not just to the behavior of the Sun as a
   gravitational lens but also to another astronomical phenomenon, the
   [748]precession of the perihelion of Mercury (a slow drift in the point
   in Mercury's elliptical orbit at which it approaches the Sun most
   closely).^[749][48]^[750][99] A [751]total eclipse of the Sun that took
   place on 29 May 1919 provided an opportunity to put his theory of
   gravitational lensing to the test, and observations performed by Sir
   [752]Arthur Eddington yielded results that were consistent with his
   calculations. Eddington's work was reported at length in newspapers
   around the world. On 7 November 1919, for example, the leading British
   newspaper, [753]The Times, printed a banner headline that read:
   "Revolution in Science – New Theory of the Universe – Newtonian Ideas
   Overthrown".^[754][100]

1921–1923: Coming to terms with fame

   [755][170px-Albert_Einstein_%28Nobel%29.png] Einstein's official
   portrait after receiving the 1921 [756]Nobel Prize for Physics

   With Eddington's eclipse observations widely reported not just in
   academic journals but by the popular press as well, Einstein became
   "perhaps the world's first celebrity scientist", a genius who had
   shattered a paradigm that had been basic to physicists' understanding
   of the universe since the seventeenth century.^[757][101]

   Einstein began his new life as an intellectual icon in America, where
   he arrived on 2 April 1921. He was welcomed to New York City by Mayor
   [758]John Francis Hylan, and then spent three weeks giving lectures and
   attending receptions.^[759][102] He spoke several times at
   [760]Columbia University and [761]Princeton, and in Washington, he
   visited the [762]White House with representatives of the [763]National
   Academy of Sciences. He returned to Europe via London, where he was the
   guest of the philosopher and statesman [764]Viscount Haldane. He used
   his time in the British capital to meet several people prominent in
   British scientific, political or intellectual life, and to deliver a
   lecture at [765]King's College.^[766][103]^[767][104] In July 1921, he
   published an essay, "My First Impression of the U.S.A.", in which he
   sought to sketch the American character, much as had [768]Alexis de
   Tocqueville in [769]Democracy in America (1835).^[770][105] He wrote of
   his transatlantic hosts in highly approving terms:
   "What strikes a visitor is the joyous, positive attitude to life ...
   The American is friendly, self-confident, optimistic, and without
   envy."^[771][106]

   In 1922, Einstein's travels were to the old world rather than the new.
   He devoted six months to a tour of Asia that saw him speaking in Japan,
   Singapore and Sri Lanka (then known as [772]Ceylon). After his first
   public lecture in Tokyo, he met [773]Emperor Yoshihito and his wife at
   the [774]Imperial Palace, with thousands of spectators thronging the
   streets in the hope of catching a glimpse of him. (In a letter to his
   sons, he wrote that Japanese people seemed to him to be generally
   modest, intelligent and considerate, and to have a true appreciation of
   art.^[775][107] But his picture of them in his diary was less
   flattering:
   "[the] intellectual needs of this nation seem to be weaker than their
   artistic ones – natural disposition?" His journal also contains views
   of China and India which were uncomplimentary. Of Chinese people, he
   wrote that "even the children are spiritless and look obtuse... It
   would be a pity if these Chinese supplant all other races. For the
   likes of us the mere thought is unspeakably
   dreary".^[776][108]^[777][109]) He was greeted with even greater
   enthusiasm on the last leg of his tour, in which he spent twelve days
   in [778]Mandatory Palestine, newly entrusted to British rule by the
   [779]League of Nations in the aftermath of the First World War.
   [780]Sir Herbert Samuel, the British High Commissioner, welcomed him
   with a degree of ceremony normally only accorded to a visiting head of
   state, including a cannon salute. One reception held in his honor was
   stormed by people determined to hear him speak: he told them that he
   was happy that Jews were beginning to be recognized as a force in the
   world.^[781][110]

   Einstein's decision to tour the eastern hemisphere in 1922 meant that
   he was unable to go to [782]Stockholm in the December of that year to
   participate in the Nobel prize ceremony. His place at the traditional
   Nobel banquet was taken by a German diplomat, who gave a speech
   praising him not only as a physicist but also as a campaigner for
   peace.^[783][111] A two-week visit to Spain that he undertook in 1923
   saw him collecting another award, a membership of the Spanish Academy
   of Sciences signified by a diploma handed to him by [784]King Alfonso
   XIII. (His Spanish trip also gave him a chance to meet a fellow Nobel
   laureate, the neuroanatomist [785]Santiago Ramón y Cajal.)^[786][112]

1922–1932: Serving the League of Nations

   [787][lossy-page1-220px-League_of_Nations_Commission_067.tif.jpg]
   Einstein at a session of the [788]International Committee on
   Intellectual Cooperation ([789]League of Nations) of which he was a
   member from 1922 to 1932

   From 1922 until 1932, with the exception of a few months in 1923 and
   1924, Einstein was a member of the Geneva-based [790]International
   Committee on Intellectual Cooperation of the [791]League of Nations, a
   group set up by the League to encourage scientists, artists, scholars,
   teachers and other people engaged in the life of the mind to work more
   closely with their counterparts in other
   countries.^[792][113]^[793][114] He was appointed as a German delegate
   rather than as a representative of Switzerland because of the
   machinations of two Catholic activists, [794]Oskar Halecki and
   [795]Giuseppe Motta. By persuading Secretary General [796]Eric Drummond
   to deny Einstein the place on the committee reserved for a Swiss
   thinker, they created an opening for [797]Gonzague de Reynold, who used
   his League of Nations position as a platform from which to promote
   traditional Catholic doctrine.^[798][115] Einstein's former physics
   professor [799]Hendrik Lorentz and the Polish chemist [800]Marie Curie
   were also members of the committee.^[801][116]

1925: Touring South America

   In March and April 1925, Einstein and his wife visited South America,
   where they spent about a week in Brazil, a week in Uruguay and a month
   in Argentina.^[802][117] Their tour was suggested by Jorge Duclout
   (1856–1927) and Mauricio Nirenstein (1877–1935)^[803][118] with the
   support of several Argentine scholars, including [804]Julio Rey Pastor,
   [805]Jakob Laub, and [806]Leopoldo Lugones. and was financed primarily
   by the Council of the [807]University of Buenos Aires and the
   Asociación Hebraica Argentina (Argentine Hebraic Association) with a
   smaller contribution from the Argentine-Germanic Cultural
   Institution.^[808][119]

1930–1931: Touring the US

   [809][180px-Albert_Einstein_writing_on_a_blackboard_in_Pasadena_%281931
   %29.jpg] Einstein in [810]Pasadena, California, 1931

   In December 1930, Einstein began another significant sojourn in the
   United States, drawn back to the US by the offer of a two month
   research fellowship at the [811]California Institute of Technology.
   Caltech supported him in his wish that he should not be exposed to
   quite as much attention from the media as he had experienced when
   visiting the US in 1921, and he therefore declined all the invitations
   to receive prizes or make speeches that his admirers poured down upon
   him. But he remained willing to allow his fans at least some of the
   time with him that they requested.^[812][120]

   After arriving in New York City, Einstein was taken to various places
   and events, including [813]Chinatown, a lunch with the editors of The
   New York Times, and a performance of Carmen at the [814]Metropolitan
   Opera, where he was cheered by the audience on his arrival. During the
   days following, he was given the keys to the city by Mayor [815]Jimmy
   Walker and met [816]Nicholas Murray Butler, the president of
   [817]Columbia University, who described Einstein as "the ruling monarch
   of the mind".^[818][121] [819]Harry Emerson Fosdick, pastor at New
   York's [820]Riverside Church, gave Einstein a tour of the church and
   showed him a full-size statue that the church made of Einstein,
   standing at the entrance.^[821][121] Also during his stay in New York,
   he joined a crowd of 15,000 people at [822]Madison Square Garden during
   a [823]Hanukkah celebration.^[824][121]
   [825][200px-Albert_Einstein_and_Charlie_Chaplin_City_Lights_premiere_19
   31.jpg] Einstein with [826]Charlie Chaplin at the [827]Hollywood
   premiere of Chaplin's [828]City Lights, January 1931

   Einstein next traveled to California, where he met [829]Caltech
   president and Nobel laureate [830]Robert A. Millikan. His friendship
   with Millikan was
   "awkward", as Millikan "had a penchant for patriotic militarism", where
   Einstein was a pronounced [831]pacifist.^[832][122] During an address
   to Caltech's students, Einstein noted that science was often inclined
   to do more harm than good.^[833][123]

   This aversion to war also led Einstein to befriend author [834]Upton
   Sinclair and film star [835]Charlie Chaplin, both noted for their
   pacifism. [836]Carl Laemmle, head of [837]Universal Studios, gave
   Einstein a tour of his studio and introduced him to Chaplin. They had
   an instant rapport, with Chaplin inviting Einstein and his wife, Elsa,
   to his home for dinner. Chaplin said Einstein's outward persona, calm
   and gentle, seemed to conceal a "highly emotional temperament", from
   which came his "extraordinary intellectual energy".^[838][124]

   Chaplin's film [839]City Lights was to premiere a few days later in
   Hollywood, and Chaplin invited Einstein and Elsa to join him as his
   special guests. [840]Walter Isaacson, Einstein's biographer, described
   this as
   "one of the most memorable scenes in the new era of
   celebrity".^[841][123] Chaplin visited Einstein at his home on a later
   trip to Berlin and recalled his "modest little flat" and the piano at
   which he had begun writing his theory. Chaplin speculated that it was
   "possibly used as kindling wood by the Nazis".^[842][125]

1933: Emigration to the US

   [843]Cartoon of Einstein, who has shed his "Pacifism" wings, standing
   next to a pillar labeled "World Peace". He is rolling up his sleeves
   and holding a sword labeled "Preparedness". Cartoon of Einstein after
   shedding his "pacifism" wings ([844]Charles R. Macauley, c. 1933)

   In February 1933, while on a visit to the United States, Einstein knew
   he could not return to Germany with the rise to power of the [845]Nazis
   under Germany's new chancellor, [846]Adolf
   Hitler.^[847][126]^[848][127]

   While at American universities in early 1933, he undertook his third
   two-month visiting professorship at the [849]California Institute of
   Technology in Pasadena. In February and March 1933, the [850]Gestapo
   repeatedly raided his family's apartment in Berlin.^[851][128] He and
   his wife Elsa returned to Europe in March, and during the trip, they
   learned that the German Reichstag had passed the [852]Enabling Act on
   23 March, transforming Hitler's government into a de facto legal
   dictatorship, and that they would not be able to proceed to Berlin.
   Later on, they heard that their cottage had been raided by the Nazis
   and Einstein's personal sailboat confiscated. Upon landing in
   [853]Antwerp, Belgium on 28 March, Einstein immediately went to the
   German consulate and surrendered his passport, formally renouncing his
   German citizenship.^[854][129] The Nazis later sold his boat and
   converted his cottage into a [855]Hitler Youth camp.^[856][130]

Refugee status

   [857][220px-Einstein%27s_landing_card_%285706142737%29.jpg] Landing
   card for Einstein's 26 May 1933 arrival in [858]Dover, England from
   [859]Ostend, Belgium,^[860][131] enroute to [861]Oxford^[862][132]

   In April 1933, Einstein discovered that the new German government had
   passed [863]laws barring Jews from holding any official positions,
   including teaching at universities.^[864][129] Historian [865]Gerald
   Holton describes how, with
   "virtually no audible protest being raised by their colleagues",
   thousands of Jewish scientists were suddenly forced to give up their
   university positions and their names were removed from the rolls of
   institutions where they were employed.^[866][133]

   A month later, Einstein's works were among those targeted by the
   [867]German Student Union in the [868]Nazi book burnings, with Nazi
   propaganda minister [869]Joseph Goebbels proclaiming, "Jewish
   intellectualism is dead."^[870][129] One German magazine included him
   in a list of enemies of the German regime with the phrase, "not yet
   hanged", offering a $5,000 bounty on his head.^[871][129]^[872][134] In
   a subsequent letter to physicist and friend [873]Max Born, who had
   already emigrated from Germany to England, Einstein wrote,
   "... I must confess that the degree of their brutality and cowardice
   came as something of a surprise."^[874][129] After moving to the US, he
   described the book burnings as a "spontaneous emotional outburst" by
   those who "shun popular enlightenment", and "more than anything else in
   the world, fear the influence of men of intellectual
   independence".^[875][135]

   Einstein was now without a permanent home, unsure where he would live
   and work, and equally worried about the fate of countless other
   scientists still in Germany. Aided by the [876]Academic Assistance
   Council, founded in April 1933 by British Liberal politician
   [877]William Beveridge to help academics escape Nazi persecution,
   Einstein was able to leave Germany.^[878][136] He rented a house in De
   Haan, Belgium, where he lived for a few months. In late July 1933, he
   visited England for about six weeks at the invitation of the British
   Member of Parliament Commander [879]Oliver Locker-Lampson, who had
   become friends with him in the preceding years.^[880][131]
   Locker-Lampson invited him to stay near his [881]Cromer home in a
   secluded wooden cabin on Roughton Heath in the Parish of [882]Roughton,
   Norfolk. To protect Einstein, Locker-Lampson had two bodyguards watch
   over him; a photo of them carrying shotguns and guarding Einstein was
   published in the Daily Herald on 24 July 1933.^[883][137]^[884][138]
   [885][220px-Churchill_and_Einstein_in_1933.jpg] [886]Winston Churchill
   and Einstein at [887]Chartwell House, 31 May 1933

   Locker-Lampson took Einstein to meet [888]Winston Churchill at his
   home, and later, [889]Austen Chamberlain and former Prime Minister
   [890]Lloyd George.^[891][139] Einstein asked them to help bring Jewish
   scientists out of Germany. British historian [892]Martin Gilbert notes
   that Churchill responded immediately, and sent his friend, physicist
   [893]Frederick Lindemann, to Germany to seek out Jewish scientists and
   place them in British universities.^[894][140] Churchill later observed
   that as a result of Germany having driven the Jews out, they had
   lowered their "technical standards" and put [895]the Allies' technology
   ahead of theirs.^[896][140]

   Einstein later contacted leaders of other nations, including Turkey's
   Prime Minister, [897]İsmet İnönü, to whom he wrote in September 1933,
   requesting placement of unemployed German-Jewish scientists. As a
   result of Einstein's letter, Jewish invitees to Turkey eventually
   totaled over "1,000 saved individuals".^[898][141]

   Locker-Lampson also submitted a bill to parliament to extend British
   citizenship to Einstein, during which period Einstein made a number of
   public appearances describing the crisis brewing in Europe.^[899][142]
   In one of his speeches he denounced Germany's treatment of Jews, while
   at the same time he introduced a bill promoting Jewish citizenship in
   Palestine, as they were being denied citizenship elsewhere.^[900][143]
   In his speech he described Einstein as a "citizen of the world" who
   should be offered a temporary shelter in the UK.^[901][note
   3]^[902][144] Both bills failed, however, and Einstein then accepted an
   earlier offer from the [903]Institute for Advanced Study, in
   [904]Princeton, New Jersey, US, to become a resident
   scholar.^[905][142]

Resident scholar at the Institute for Advanced Study

   [906][170px-Einstein-formal_portrait-35_%28cropped%29.jpg] Portrait of
   Einstein taken in 1935 at [907]Princeton

   On 3 October 1933, Einstein delivered a speech on the importance of
   academic freedom before a packed audience at the [908]Royal Albert Hall
   in London, with [909]The Times reporting he was wildly cheered
   throughout.^[910][136] Four days later he returned to the US and took
   up a position at the [911]Institute for Advanced
   Study,^[912][142]^[913][145] noted for having become a refuge for
   scientists fleeing Nazi Germany.^[914][146] At the time, most American
   universities, including Harvard, Princeton and Yale, had minimal or no
   Jewish faculty or students, as a result of their [915]Jewish quotas,
   which lasted until the late 1940s.^[916][146]

   Einstein was still undecided about his future. He had offers from
   several European universities, including [917]Christ Church, Oxford,
   where he stayed for three short periods between May 1931 and June
   1933^[918][132] and was offered a five-year research [919]fellowship
   (called a "[920]studentship" at Christ Church),^[921][147]^[922][148]
   but in 1935, he arrived at the decision to remain permanently in the
   United States and apply for citizenship.^[923][142]^[924][149]

   Einstein's affiliation with the Institute for Advanced Study would last
   until his death in 1955.^[925][150] He was one of the four first
   selected (along with [926]John von Neumann, [927]Kurt Gödel, and
   [928]Hermann Weyl^[929][151]) at the new Institute. He soon developed a
   close friendship with Gödel; the two would take long walks together
   discussing their work. [930]Bruria Kaufman, his assistant, later became
   a physicist. During this period, Einstein tried to develop a
   [931]unified field theory and to refute the [932]accepted
   interpretation of [933]quantum physics, both unsuccessfully. He lived
   in Princeton at his home from 1935 onwards. The [934]Albert Einstein
   House was made a [935]National Historic Landmark in 1976.

World War II and the Manhattan Project

   See also: [936]Einstein–Szilárd letter

   [937][310px-Einstein-Roosevelt-letter.png] Facsimile of the
   [938]Einstein–Szilard letter

   In 1939, a group of Hungarian scientists that included émigré physicist
   [939]Leó Szilárd attempted to alert [940]Washington to ongoing Nazi
   atomic bomb research. The group's warnings were discounted. Einstein
   and Szilárd, along with other refugees such as [941]Edward Teller and
   [942]Eugene Wigner,
   "regarded it as their responsibility to alert Americans to the
   possibility that German scientists might win the [943]race to build an
   atomic bomb, and to warn that Hitler would be more than willing to
   resort to such a weapon."^[944][152]^[945][153] To make certain the US
   was aware of the danger, in July 1939, a few months before the
   beginning of World War II in Europe, Szilárd and Wigner visited
   Einstein to explain the possibility of atomic bombs, which Einstein, a
   pacifist, said he had never considered.^[946][154] He was asked to lend
   his support by writing [947]a letter, with Szilárd, to President
   [948]Roosevelt, recommending the US pay attention and engage in its own
   nuclear weapons research.

   The letter is believed to be
   "arguably the key stimulus for the U.S. adoption of serious
   investigations into nuclear weapons on the eve of the U.S. entry into
   World War II".^[949][155] In addition to the letter, Einstein used his
   connections with the [950]Belgian royal family^[951][156] and the
   Belgian queen mother to get access with a personal envoy to the White
   House's Oval Office. Some say that as a result of Einstein's letter and
   his meetings with Roosevelt, the US entered the "race" to develop the
   bomb, drawing on its "immense material, financial, and scientific
   resources" to initiate the [952]Manhattan Project.

   For Einstein,
   "war was a disease ... [and] he called for resistance to war." By
   signing the letter to Roosevelt, some argue he went against his
   pacifist principles.^[953][157] In 1954, a year before his death,
   Einstein said to his old friend, [954]Linus Pauling, "I made one great
   mistake in my life—when I signed the letter to President Roosevelt
   recommending that atom bombs be made; but there was some
   justification—the danger that the Germans would make
   them ..."^[955][158] In 1955, Einstein and ten other intellectuals and
   scientists, including British philosopher [956]Bertrand Russell, signed
   [957]a manifesto highlighting the danger of nuclear weapons.^[958][159]
   In 1960 Einstein was included posthumously as a charter member of the
   [959]World Academy of Art and Science (WAAS),^[960][160] an
   organization founded by distinguished scientists and intellectuals who
   committed themselves to the responsible and ethical advances of
   science, particularly in light of the development of nuclear weapons.

US citizenship

   [961][220px-Citizen-Einstein.jpg] Einstein accepting a [962]US
   citizenship certificate from judge [963]Phillip Forman in 1940

   Einstein became an American citizen in 1940. Not long after settling
   into his career at the Institute for Advanced Study in Princeton, New
   Jersey, he expressed his appreciation of the [964]meritocracy in
   American culture compared to Europe. He recognized the "right of
   individuals to say and think what they pleased" without social
   barriers. As a result, individuals were encouraged, he said, to be more
   creative, a trait he valued from his early education.^[965][161]

   Einstein joined the [966]National Association for the Advancement of
   Colored People (NAACP) in Princeton, where he campaigned for the
   [967]civil rights of African Americans. He considered racism America's
   "worst disease",^[968][134]^[969][162] seeing it as
   "handed down from one generation to the next".^[970][163] As part of
   his involvement, he corresponded with civil rights activist [971]W. E.
   B. Du Bois and was prepared to testify on his behalf during his trial
   as an alleged foreign agent in 1951.^[972][164] When Einstein offered
   to be a character witness for Du Bois, the judge decided to drop the
   case.^[973][165]

   In 1946, Einstein visited [974]Lincoln University in Pennsylvania, a
   [975]historically black college, where he was awarded an honorary
   degree. Lincoln was the first university in the United States to grant
   college degrees to African Americans; alumni include [976]Langston
   Hughes and [977]Thurgood Marshall. Einstein gave a speech about racism
   in America, adding,
   "I do not intend to be quiet about it."^[978][166] A resident of
   Princeton recalls that Einstein had once paid the college tuition for a
   black student.^[979][165] Einstein has said, "Being a Jew myself,
   perhaps I can understand and empathize with how black people feel as
   victims of discrimination".^[980][162]

Personal views

Political views

   Main article: [981]Political views of Albert Einstein

   [982]Casual group shot of four men and two women standing on a brick
   pavement. Albert Einstein and [983]Elsa Einstein arriving in New York
   in 1921. Accompanying them are Zionist leaders [984]Chaim Weizmann
   (future president of Israel), Weizmann's wife [985]Vera Weizmann,
   [986]Menahem Ussishkin, and Ben-Zion Mossinson.

   In 1918, Einstein was one of the signatories of the founding
   proclamation of the [987]German Democratic Party, a liberal
   party.^[988][167]^[989][168] Later in his life, Einstein's political
   view was in favor of [990]socialism and critical of capitalism, which
   he detailed in his essays such as "[991]Why
   Socialism?".^[992][169]^[993][170] His opinions on the [994]Bolsheviks
   also changed with time. In 1925, he criticized them for not having a
   "well-regulated system of government" and called their rule a "regime
   of terror and a tragedy in human history". He later adopted a more
   moderated view, criticizing their methods but praising them, which is
   shown by his 1929 remark on [995]Vladimir Lenin:

     In Lenin I honor a man, who in total sacrifice of his own person has
     committed his entire energy to realizing social justice. I do not
     find his methods advisable. One thing is certain, however: men like
     him are the guardians and renewers of mankind's
     conscience.^[996][171]

   Einstein offered and was called on to give judgments and opinions on
   matters often unrelated to theoretical physics or
   mathematics.^[997][142] He strongly advocated the idea of a democratic
   [998]global government that would check the power of nation-states in
   the framework of a world federation.^[999][172] He wrote
   "I advocate world government because I am convinced that there is no
   other possible way of eliminating the most terrible danger in which man
   has ever found himself."^[1000][173] The FBI created a secret dossier
   on Einstein in 1932; by the time of his death, it was 1,427 pages
   long.^[1001][174]

   Einstein was deeply impressed by [1002]Mahatma Gandhi, with whom he
   corresponded. He described Gandhi as
   "a role model for the generations to come".^[1003][175] The initial
   connection was established on 27 September 1931, when [1004]Wilfrid
   Israel took his Indian guest [1005]V. A. Sundaram to meet his friend
   Einstein at his summer home in the town of Caputh. Sundaram was
   Gandhi's disciple and special envoy, whom Wilfrid Israel met while
   visiting India and visiting the Indian leader's home in 1925. During
   the visit, Einstein wrote a short letter to Gandhi that was delivered
   to him through his envoy, and Gandhi responded quickly with his own
   letter. Although in the end Einstein and Gandhi were unable to meet as
   they had hoped, the direct connection between them was established
   through Wilfrid Israel.^[1006][176]

Relationship with Zionism

   Main article: [1007]Political views of Albert Einstein § Zionism

   [1008][170px-Albert_Einstein_Head.jpg] Einstein in 1947

   Einstein was a figurehead leader in the establishment of the
   [1009]Hebrew University of Jerusalem,^[1010][177] which opened in
   1925.^[1011][178] Earlier, in 1921, he was asked by the biochemist and
   president of the [1012]World Zionist Organization, [1013]Chaim
   Weizmann, to help raise funds for the planned university.^[1014][179]
   He made suggestions for the creation of an Institute of Agriculture, a
   Chemical Institute and an Institute of Microbiology in order to fight
   the various ongoing epidemics such as [1015]malaria, which he called an
   "evil" that was undermining a third of the country's
   development.^[1016][180] He also promoted the establishment of an
   Oriental Studies Institute, to include language courses given in both
   Hebrew and Arabic.^[1017][181]

   Einstein was not a [1018]nationalist and opposed the creation of an
   independent Jewish state.^[1019][182] He felt that the waves of
   arriving Jews of the [1020]Aliyah could live alongside existing Arabs
   in [1021]Palestine. The state of [1022]Israel was established without
   his help in 1948; Einstein was limited to a marginal role in the
   [1023]Zionist movement.^[1024][183] Upon the death of Israeli president
   Weizmann in November 1952, Prime Minister [1025]David Ben-Gurion
   offered Einstein the largely ceremonial position of [1026]President of
   Israel at the urging of [1027]Ezriel Carlebach.^[1028][184]^[1029][185]
   The offer was presented by Israel's ambassador in Washington,
   [1030]Abba Eban, who explained that the offer
   "embodies the deepest respect which the Jewish people can repose in any
   of its sons".^[1031][186] Einstein wrote that he was "deeply moved",
   but "at once saddened and ashamed" that he could not accept
   it.^[1032][186]

Religious and philosophical views

   Main article: [1033]Religious and philosophical views of Albert
   Einstein

   Opening of Einstein's speech (11 April 1943) for the United Jewish
   Appeal (recording by Radio Universidad Nacional de La Plata, Argentina)
     __________________________________________________________________

   "Ladies (coughs) and gentlemen, our age is proud of the progress it has
   made in man's intellectual development. The search and striving for
   truth and knowledge is one of the highest of man's qualities ..."

   Per [1034]Lee Smolin,
   "I believe what allowed Einstein to achieve so much was primarily a
   moral quality. He simply cared far more than most of his colleagues
   that the laws of physics have to explain everything in nature
   coherently and consistently."^[1035][187] Einstein expounded his
   spiritual outlook in a wide array of writings and
   interviews.^[1036][188] He said he had sympathy for the impersonal
   [1037]pantheistic God of [1038]Baruch Spinoza's philosophy.^[1039][189]
   He did not believe in a [1040]personal god who concerns himself with
   fates and actions of human beings, a view which he described as
   naïve.^[1041][190] He clarified, however, that "I am not an
   atheist",^[1042][191] preferring to call himself an
   agnostic,^[1043][192]^[1044][193] or a "deeply religious
   nonbeliever".^[1045][190] He wrote that "A spirit is manifest in the
   laws of the universe—a spirit vastly superior to that of man, and one
   in the face of which we with our modest powers must feel humble. In
   this way the pursuit of science leads to a religious feeling of a
   special sort."^[1046][194]

   Einstein was primarily affiliated with non-religious [1047]humanist and
   [1048]Ethical Culture groups in both the UK and US. He served on the
   advisory board of the [1049]First Humanist Society of New
   York,^[1050][195] and was an honorary associate of the
   [1051]Rationalist Association, which publishes [1052]New Humanist in
   Britain. For the 75th anniversary of the [1053]New York Society for
   Ethical Culture, he stated that the idea of Ethical Culture embodied
   his personal conception of what is most valuable and enduring in
   religious idealism. He observed,
   "Without 'ethical culture' there is no salvation for
   humanity."^[1054][196]

   In a German-language letter to philosopher [1055]Eric Gutkind, dated 3
   January 1954, Einstein wrote:

     [1056]The word God is for me nothing more than the expression and
     product of human weaknesses, the Bible a collection of honorable,
     but still primitive legends which are nevertheless pretty childish.
     No interpretation no matter how subtle can (for me) change this. ...
     For me the [1057]Jewish religion like all other religions is an
     incarnation of the most childish superstitions. And the [1058]Jewish
     people to whom I gladly belong and with whose mentality I have a
     deep affinity have no different quality for me than all other
     people. ... I cannot see anything '[1059]chosen' about
     them.^[1060][197]

   Einstein had been sympathetic toward vegetarianism for a long time. In
   a letter in 1930 to Hermann Huth, vice-president of the [1061]German
   Vegetarian Federation (Deutsche Vegetarier-Bund), he wrote:

     Although I have been prevented by outward circumstances from
     observing a strictly vegetarian diet, I have long been an adherent
     to the cause in principle. Besides agreeing with the aims of
     vegetarianism for aesthetic and moral reasons, it is my view that a
     vegetarian manner of living by its purely physical effect on the
     human temperament would most beneficially influence the lot of
     mankind.^[1062][198]

   He became a vegetarian himself only during the last part of his life.
   In March 1954 he wrote in a letter:
   "So I am living without fats, without meat, without fish, but am
   feeling quite well this way. It almost seems to me that man was not
   born to be a carnivore."^[1063][199]

Love of music

   [1064][230px-Wanda_von_Debschitz-Kunowski_Albert_Einstein_beim_Geigensp
   iel_1927.jpg] Einstein playing the violin, 1927

   Einstein developed an appreciation for music at an early age. In his
   late journals he wrote:

     If I were not a physicist, I would probably be a musician. I often
     think in music. I live my daydreams in music. I see my life in terms
     of music ... I get most joy in life out of
     music.^[1065][200]^[1066][201]

   His mother played the piano reasonably well and wanted her son to learn
   the violin, not only to instill in him a love of music but also to help
   him assimilate into [1067]German culture. According to conductor
   [1068]Leon Botstein, Einstein began playing when he was 5. However, he
   did not enjoy it at that age.^[1069][202]

   When he turned 13, he discovered [1070]Mozart's [1071]violin sonatas,
   whereupon he became enamored of Mozart's compositions and studied music
   more willingly. Einstein taught himself to play without "ever
   practicing systematically". He said that
   "love is a better teacher than a sense of duty".^[1072][202] At the age
   of 17, he was heard by a school examiner in Aarau while playing
   [1073]Beethoven's [1074]violin sonatas. The examiner stated afterward
   that his playing was "remarkable and revealing of 'great insight'".
   What struck the examiner, writes Botstein, was that Einstein "displayed
   a deep love of the music, a quality that was and remains in short
   supply. Music possessed an unusual meaning for this
   student."^[1075][202]

   Music took on a pivotal and permanent role in Einstein's life from that
   period on. Although the idea of becoming a professional musician
   himself was not on his mind at any time, among those with whom Einstein
   played [1076]chamber music were a few professionals, including Kurt
   Appelbaum, and he performed for private audiences and friends. Chamber
   music had also become a regular part of his social life while living in
   Bern, Zurich, and Berlin, where he played with Max Planck and his son,
   among others. He is sometimes erroneously credited as the editor of the
   1937 edition of the [1077]Köchel catalog of Mozart's work; that edition
   was prepared by [1078]Alfred Einstein, who may have been a distant
   relation.^[1079][203]^[1080][204]

   In 1931, while engaged in research at the California Institute of
   Technology, he visited the Zoellner family conservatory in Los Angeles,
   where he played some of [1081]Beethoven and Mozart's works with members
   of the [1082]Zoellner Quartet.^[1083][205]^[1084][206] Near the end of
   his life, when the young [1085]Juilliard Quartet visited him in
   Princeton, he played his violin with them, and the quartet was
   "impressed by Einstein's level of coordination and
   intonation".^[1086][202]

Death

   On 17 April 1955, Einstein experienced [1087]internal bleeding caused
   by the rupture of an [1088]abdominal aortic aneurysm, which had
   previously been reinforced surgically by [1089]Rudolph Nissen in
   1948.^[1090][207] He took the draft of a speech he was preparing for a
   television appearance commemorating the state of Israel's seventh
   anniversary with him to the hospital, but he did not live to complete
   it.^[1091][208]

   Einstein refused surgery, saying,
   "I want to go when I want. It is tasteless to prolong life
   artificially. I have done my share; it is time to go. I will do it
   elegantly."^[1092][209] He died in the [1093]Princeton Hospital early
   the next morning at the age of 76, having continued to work until near
   the end.^[1094][210]

   During the autopsy, the pathologist [1095]Thomas Stoltz Harvey removed
   [1096]Einstein's brain for preservation without the permission of his
   family, in the hope that the [1097]neuroscience of the future would be
   able to discover what made Einstein so intelligent.^[1098][211]
   Einstein's remains were cremated in [1099]Trenton, New
   Jersey,^[1100][212] and his ashes were scattered at an undisclosed
   location.^[1101][213]^[1102][214]

   In a memorial lecture delivered on 13 December 1965 at [1103]UNESCO
   headquarters, nuclear physicist [1104]J. Robert Oppenheimer summarized
   his [1105]impression of Einstein as a person:
   "He was almost wholly without sophistication and wholly without
   worldliness ... There was always with him a wonderful purity at once
   childlike and profoundly stubborn."^[1106][215]

   Einstein bequeathed his personal archives, library, and intellectual
   assets to the [1107]Hebrew University of Jerusalem in
   Israel.^[1108][216]

Scientific career

   Throughout his life, Einstein published hundreds of books and
   articles.^[1109][17]^[1110][217] He published more than 300 scientific
   papers and 150 non-scientific ones.^[1111][11]^[1112][217] On 5
   December 2014, universities and archives announced the release of
   Einstein's papers, comprising more than 30,000 unique
   documents.^[1113][218]^[1114][219] In addition to the work he did by
   himself he also collaborated with other scientists on additional
   projects including the [1115]Bose–Einstein statistics, the
   [1116]Einstein refrigerator and others.^[1117][220]^[1118][221]

Statistical mechanics

Thermodynamic fluctuations and statistical physics

   Main articles: [1119]Statistical mechanics, [1120]thermal fluctuations,
   and [1121]statistical physics

   Einstein's first paper^[1122][77]^[1123][222] submitted in 1900 to
   [1124]Annalen der Physik was on [1125]capillary attraction. It was
   published in 1901 with the title "Folgerungen aus den
   Capillaritätserscheinungen", which translates as "Conclusions from the
   capillarity phenomena". Two papers he published in 1902–1903
   (thermodynamics) attempted to interpret [1126]atomic phenomena from a
   statistical point of view. These papers were the foundation for the
   1905 paper on Brownian motion, which showed that Brownian movement can
   be construed as firm evidence that molecules exist. His research in
   1903 and 1904 was mainly concerned with the effect of finite atomic
   size on diffusion phenomena.^[1127][222]

Theory of critical opalescence

   Main article: [1128]Critical opalescence

   Einstein returned to the problem of thermodynamic fluctuations, giving
   a treatment of the density variations in a fluid at its critical point.
   Ordinarily the density fluctuations are controlled by the second
   derivative of the free energy with respect to the density. At the
   critical point, this derivative is zero, leading to large fluctuations.
   The effect of density fluctuations is that light of all wavelengths is
   scattered, making the fluid look milky white. Einstein relates this to
   [1129]Rayleigh scattering, which is what happens when the fluctuation
   size is much smaller than the wavelength, and which explains why the
   sky is blue.^[1130][223] Einstein quantitatively derived critical
   opalescence from a treatment of density fluctuations, and demonstrated
   how both the effect and Rayleigh scattering originate from the
   atomistic constitution of matter.

1905 – Annus Mirabilis papers

   The [1131]Annus Mirabilis papers are four articles pertaining to the
   photoelectric effect (which gave rise to [1132]quantum theory),
   [1133]Brownian motion, the [1134]special theory of relativity, and
   [1135]E = mc^2 that Einstein published in the Annalen der Physik
   scientific journal in 1905. These four works contributed substantially
   to the foundation of [1136]modern physics^[[1137]broken anchor] and
   changed views on [1138]space, time, and [1139]matter. The four papers
   are:

   Title (translated) Area of focus Received Published Significance
   "On a Heuristic Viewpoint Concerning the Production and Transformation
   of Light"^[1140][224] Photoelectric effect 18 March 9 June Resolved an
   unsolved puzzle by suggesting that energy is exchanged only in discrete
   amounts ([1141]quanta).^[1142][225] This idea was pivotal to the early
   development of quantum theory.^[1143][226]
   "On the Motion of Small Particles Suspended in a Stationary Liquid, as
   Required by the Molecular Kinetic Theory of Heat"^[1144][227]
   [1145]Brownian motion 11 May 18 July Explained empirical evidence for
   the [1146]atomic theory, supporting the application of
   [1147]statistical physics.
   "On the Electrodynamics of Moving Bodies"^[1148][228] [1149]Special
   relativity 30 June 26 September Reconciled [1150]Maxwell's equations
   for electricity and magnetism with the laws of mechanics by introducing
   changes to mechanics, resulting from analysis based on empirical
   evidence that the speed of light is independent of the motion of the
   observer.^[1151][229]^[[1152]specify] Discredited the concept of a
   "[1153]luminiferous ether".^[1154][230]
   "Does the Inertia of a Body Depend Upon Its Energy
   Content?"^[1155][231] [1156]Matter–energy equivalence 27 September 21
   November Equivalence of matter and energy, E = mc^2, the existence of
   "[1157]rest energy", and the basis of nuclear energy.

Special relativity

   Main article: [1158]History of special relativity

   Einstein's "Zur Elektrodynamik bewegter Körper"^[1159][228] ("On the
   Electrodynamics of Moving Bodies") was received on 30 June 1905 and
   published 26 September of that same year. It reconciled conflicts
   between [1160]Maxwell's equations (the laws of electricity and
   magnetism) and the laws of Newtonian mechanics by introducing changes
   to the laws of mechanics.^[1161][232] Observationally, the effects of
   these changes are most apparent at high speeds (where objects are
   moving at speeds close to the [1162]speed of light). The theory
   developed in this paper later became known as Einstein's special theory
   of relativity.

   This paper predicted that, when measured in the frame of a relatively
   moving observer, a clock carried by a moving body would appear to
   [1163]slow down, and the body itself would [1164]contract in its
   direction of motion. This paper also argued that the idea of a
   [1165]luminiferous aether—one of the leading theoretical entities in
   physics at the time—was superfluous.^[1166][note 4]

   In his paper on [1167]mass–energy equivalence, Einstein produced
   E = mc^2 as a consequence of his special relativity
   equations.^[1168][233] Einstein's 1905 work on relativity remained
   controversial for many years, but was accepted by leading physicists,
   starting with [1169]Max Planck.^[1170][note 5]^[1171][234]

   Einstein originally framed special relativity in terms of
   [1172]kinematics (the study of moving bodies). In 1908, [1173]Hermann
   Minkowski reinterpreted special relativity in geometric terms as a
   theory of [1174]spacetime. Einstein adopted Minkowski's formalism in
   his 1915 [1175]general theory of relativity.^[1176][235]

General relativity

General relativity and the equivalence principle

   Main article: [1177]History of general relativity

   See also: [1178]Theory of relativity and [1179]Einstein field equations

   [1180]Black circle covering the sun, rays visible around it, in a dark
   sky. [1181]Eddington's photo of a [1182]solar eclipse

   [1183]General relativity (GR) is a [1184]theory of gravitation that was
   developed by Einstein between 1907 and 1915. According to it, the
   observed gravitational attraction between masses results from the
   warping of [1185]spacetime by those masses. General relativity has
   developed into an essential tool in modern [1186]astrophysics; it
   provides the foundation for the current understanding of [1187]black
   holes, regions of space where gravitational attraction is so strong
   that not even light can escape.^[1188][236]

   As Einstein later said, the reason for the development of general
   relativity was that the preference of inertial motions within
   [1189]special relativity was unsatisfactory, while a theory which from
   the outset prefers no state of motion (even accelerated ones) should
   appear more satisfactory.^[1190][237] Consequently, in 1907 he
   published an article on acceleration under special relativity. In that
   article titled "On the Relativity Principle and the Conclusions Drawn
   from It", he argued that [1191]free fall is really inertial motion, and
   that for a free-falling observer the rules of special relativity must
   apply. This argument is called the [1192]equivalence principle. In the
   same article, Einstein also predicted the phenomena of
   [1193]gravitational time dilation, [1194]gravitational redshift and
   [1195]gravitational lensing.^[1196][238]^[1197][239]

   In 1911, Einstein published another article "On the Influence of
   Gravitation on the Propagation of Light" expanding on the 1907 article,
   in which he estimated the amount of deflection of light by massive
   bodies. Thus, the theoretical prediction of general relativity could
   for the first time be tested experimentally.^[1198][240]

Gravitational waves

   In 1916, Einstein predicted [1199]gravitational
   waves,^[1200][241]^[1201][242] ripples in the [1202]curvature of
   spacetime which propagate as [1203]waves, traveling outward from the
   source, transporting energy as gravitational radiation. The existence
   of gravitational waves is possible under general relativity due to its
   [1204]Lorentz invariance which brings the concept of a finite speed of
   propagation of the physical interactions of gravity with it. By
   contrast, gravitational waves cannot exist in the [1205]Newtonian
   theory of gravitation, which postulates that the physical interactions
   of gravity propagate at infinite speed.

   The first, indirect, detection of gravitational waves came in the 1970s
   through observation of a pair of closely orbiting [1206]neutron stars,
   [1207]PSR B1913+16.^[1208][243] The explanation for the decay in their
   orbital period was that they were emitting gravitational
   waves.^[1209][243]^[1210][244] Einstein's prediction was confirmed on
   11 February 2016, when researchers at [1211]LIGO published the
   [1212]first observation of gravitational waves,^[1213][245] detected on
   Earth on 14 September 2015, nearly one hundred years after the
   prediction.^[1214][243]^[1215][246]^[1216][247]^[1217][248]^[1218][249]

Hole argument and Entwurf theory

   While developing general relativity, Einstein became confused about the
   [1219]gauge invariance in the theory. He formulated an argument that
   led him to conclude that a general relativistic field theory is
   impossible. He gave up looking for fully generally covariant tensor
   equations and searched for equations that would be invariant under
   general linear transformations only.^[1220][250]

   In June 1913, the Entwurf ('draft') theory was the result of these
   investigations. As its name suggests, it was a sketch of a theory, less
   elegant and more difficult than general relativity, with the equations
   of motion supplemented by additional gauge fixing conditions. After
   more than two years of intensive work, Einstein realized that the
   [1221]hole argument was mistaken^[1222][251] and abandoned the theory
   in November 1915.

Physical cosmology

   Main article: [1223]Physical cosmology

   [1224][220px-MillikanLemaitreEinstein.jpg] [1225]Robert A. Millikan,
   [1226]Georges Lemaître and Einstein at the [1227]California Institute
   of Technology in January 1933

   In 1917, Einstein applied the general theory of relativity to the
   structure of the universe as a whole.^[1228][252] He discovered that
   the general field equations predicted a universe that was dynamic,
   either contracting or expanding. As observational evidence for a
   dynamic universe was lacking at the time, Einstein introduced a new
   term, the [1229]cosmological constant, into the field equations, in
   order to allow the theory to predict a static universe. The modified
   field equations predicted a static universe of closed curvature, in
   accordance with Einstein's understanding of [1230]Mach's principle in
   these years. This model became known as the Einstein World or
   [1231]Einstein's static universe.^[1232][253]^[1233][254]

   Following the discovery of the recession of the galaxies by [1234]Edwin
   Hubble in 1929, Einstein abandoned his static model of the universe,
   and proposed two dynamic models of the cosmos, the
   [1235]Friedmann–Einstein universe of 1931^[1236][255]^[1237][256] and
   the [1238]Einstein–de Sitter universe of 1932.^[1239][257]^[1240][258]
   In each of these models, Einstein discarded the cosmological constant,
   claiming that it was "in any case theoretically
   unsatisfactory".^[1241][255]^[1242][256]^[1243][259]

   In many Einstein biographies, it is claimed that Einstein referred to
   the cosmological constant in later years as his "biggest blunder",
   based on a letter [1244]George Gamow claimed to have received from him.
   The astrophysicist [1245]Mario Livio has cast doubt on this
   claim.^[1246][260]

   In late 2013, a team led by the Irish physicist [1247]Cormac
   O'Raifeartaigh discovered evidence that, shortly after learning of
   Hubble's observations of the recession of the galaxies, Einstein
   considered a [1248]steady-state model of the
   universe.^[1249][261]^[1250][262] In a hitherto overlooked manuscript,
   apparently written in early 1931, Einstein explored a model of the
   expanding universe in which the density of matter remains constant due
   to a continuous creation of matter, a process that he associated with
   the cosmological constant.^[1251][263]^[1252][264] As he stated in the
   paper,
   "In what follows, I would like to draw attention to a solution to
   equation (1) that can account for Hubbel's [[1253]sic] facts, and in
   which the density is constant over time [...] If one considers a
   physically bounded volume, particles of matter will be continually
   leaving it. For the density to remain constant, new particles of matter
   must be continually formed in the volume from space."

   It thus appears that Einstein considered a [1254]steady-state model of
   the expanding universe many years before Hoyle, Bondi and
   Gold.^[1255][265]^[1256][266] However, Einstein's steady-state model
   contained a fundamental flaw and he quickly abandoned the
   idea.^[1257][263]^[1258][264]^[1259][267]

Energy momentum pseudotensor

   Main article: [1260]Stress–energy–momentum pseudotensor

   General relativity includes a dynamical spacetime, so it is difficult
   to see how to identify the conserved energy and momentum.
   [1261]Noether's theorem allows these quantities to be determined from a
   [1262]Lagrangian with [1263]translation invariance, but [1264]general
   covariance makes translation invariance into something of a [1265]gauge
   symmetry. The energy and momentum derived within general relativity by
   [1266]Noether's prescriptions do not make a real tensor for this
   reason.^[1267][268]

   Einstein argued that this is true for a fundamental reason: the
   gravitational field could be made to vanish by a choice of coordinates.
   He maintained that the non-covariant energy momentum pseudotensor was,
   in fact, the best description of the energy momentum distribution in a
   gravitational field. While the use of non-covariant objects like
   pseudotensors was criticized by [1268]Erwin Schrödinger and others,
   Einstein's approach has been echoed by physicists including [1269]Lev
   Landau and [1270]Evgeny Lifshitz.^[1271][269]

Wormholes

   In 1935, Einstein collaborated with [1272]Nathan Rosen to produce a
   model of a [1273]wormhole, often called [1274]Einstein–Rosen
   bridges.^[1275][14]^[1276][270] His motivation was to model elementary
   particles with charge as a solution of gravitational field equations,
   in line with the program outlined in the paper "Do Gravitational Fields
   play an Important Role in the Constitution of the Elementary
   Particles?". These solutions cut and pasted [1277]Schwarzschild black
   holes to make a bridge between two patches. Because these solutions
   included spacetime curvature without the presence of a physical body,
   Einstein and Rosen suggested that they could provide the beginnings of
   a theory that avoided the notion of point particles. However, it was
   later found that Einstein–Rosen bridges are not stable.^[1278][271]

Einstein–Cartan theory

   Main article: [1279]Einstein–Cartan theory

   [1280]Einstein, sitting at a table, looks up from the papers he is
   reading and into the camera. Einstein at his office, [1281]University
   of Berlin, 1920

   In order to incorporate spinning point particles into general
   relativity, the affine connection needed to be generalized to include
   an antisymmetric part, called the [1282]torsion. This modification was
   made by Einstein and Cartan in the 1920s.

Equations of motion

   Main article: [1283]Einstein–Infeld–Hoffmann equations

   In general relativity, gravitational force is reimagined as curvature
   of [1284]spacetime. A curved path like an orbit is not the result of a
   force deflecting a body from an ideal straight-line path, but rather
   the body's attempt to fall freely through a background that is itself
   curved by the presence of other masses. A remark by [1285]John
